									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p><b>Greenpeace</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental</a><sup id="cite_ref-UN_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmental</a> organization with offices in over 40 countries and with an international coordinating body in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GPI_world_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPI_world-4">[4]</a></sup> Founded by <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> and US ex-pat environmental activists in 1971, Greenpeace states its goal is to "ensure the ability of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> to nurture <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> in all its <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diversity</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-GPI-FAQ_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPI-FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup> and focuses its campaigning on worldwide issues such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">commercial whaling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> issues. It uses <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ecotage" title="Ecotage">ecotage</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> to achieve its goals. The global organization does not accept funding from governments, corporations, or political parties, relying on 2.9 million individual supporters and foundation grants.<sup id="cite_ref-HBS_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBS-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Annual_report_2011_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual_report_2011-8">[8]</a></sup> Greenpeace has a <a href="/wiki/List_of_organizations_with_consultative_status_to_the_United_Nations_Economic_and_Social_Council" title="List of organizations with consultative status to the United Nations Economic and Social Council">general consultative status</a> with the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Economic_and_Social_Council" title="United Nations Economic and Social Council">United Nations Economic and Social Council</a><sup id="cite_ref-ecosoc_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecosoc-9">[9]</a></sup> and is a founding member<sup id="cite_ref-INGO_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INGO-10">[10]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Non-Governmental_Organisations_Accountability_Charter" title="International Non-Governmental Organisations Accountability Charter">INGO Accountability Charter</a>; an international non-governmental organization that intends to foster accountability and transparency of non-governmental organizations.</p>
<p>Greenpeace is known for its direct actions and has been described as the most visible environmental organization in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-GP_study_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GP_study-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cbc_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc-12">[12]</a></sup> Greenpeace has raised environmental issues to public knowledge,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and influenced both the private and the public sector.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adidas.2C_Clarks.2C_Nike_and_Timberland_agree_moratorium_on_illegal_Amazon_leather_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adidas.2C_Clarks.2C_Nike_and_Timberland_agree_moratorium_on_illegal_Amazon_leather-17">[17]</a></sup> Greenpeace has also been a source of controversy;<sup id="cite_ref-autonomy_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonomy-18">[18]</a></sup> its motives and methods (some of the latter being illegal) have received criticism,<sup id="cite_ref-moore_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moore-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> including an open letter from more than 100 Nobel laureates urging Greenpeace to end its campaign against <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a> (GMOs).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[21]</a></sup> The organization's direct actions have sparked legal actions against Greenpeace <a href="/wiki/Activist" class="mw-redirect" title="Activist">activists</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> such as fines and suspended sentences for destroying a test plot of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified</a> wheat<sup id="cite_ref-oregon_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oregon-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-smh_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smh-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-canberra_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canberra-26">[26]</a></sup> and damaging the <a href="/wiki/Nazca_Lines" title="Nazca Lines">Nazca Lines</a>, a UN World Heritage site in Peru.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_Nazca_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_Nazca-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AmchitkaAlaskaLoc.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/AmchitkaAlaskaLoc.png/220px-AmchitkaAlaskaLoc.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/AmchitkaAlaskaLoc.png 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="219" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AmchitkaAlaskaLoc.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Location of Amchitka island in Alaska.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the late 1960s, the U.S. had plans for an underground <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapon test">nuclear weapon test</a> in the tectonically unstable island of <a href="/wiki/Amchitka" title="Amchitka">Amchitka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>. Because of the <a href="/wiki/1964_Alaska_earthquake" title="1964 Alaska earthquake">1964 Alaska earthquake</a>, the plans raised some concerns of the test triggering earthquakes and causing a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>. A 1969 demonstration of 7,000<sup id="cite_ref-1969_news_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1969_news-28">[28]</a></sup> people blocked a major U.S.–Canada border crossing in British Columbia, carrying signs reading "Don't Make A Wave. It's Your Fault If Our Fault Goes".<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> The protests did not stop the U.S. from detonating the bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>While no earthquake or tsunami followed the test, the opposition grew when the U.S. announced they would detonate a bomb five times more powerful than the first one. Among the opposers were <a href="/wiki/Jim_Bohlen" title="Jim Bohlen">Jim Bohlen</a>, a veteran who had served in the U.S. Navy, and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Stowe" title="Irving Stowe">Irving Stowe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Stowe" title="Dorothy Stowe">Dorothy Stowe</a>, who had recently become <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quakers</a>. As members of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Club_Canada" title="Sierra Club Canada">Sierra Club Canada</a>, they were frustrated by the lack of action by the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> From Irving Stowe, Jim Bohlen learned of a form of <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_resistance" title="Nonviolent resistance">passive resistance</a>, "bearing witness", where objectionable activity is protested simply by mere presence.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> Jim Bohlen's wife Marie came up with the idea to sail to Amchitka, inspired by the anti-nuclear voyages of <a href="/wiki/Albert_Bigelow" title="Albert Bigelow">Albert Bigelow</a> in 1958. The idea ended up in the press and was linked to The Sierra Club.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> The Sierra Club did not like this connection and in 1970 The <a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Make_a_Wave_Committee" title="Don't Make a Wave Committee">Don't Make a Wave Committee</a> was established for the protest. Early meetings were held in the Shaughnessy home of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hunter_(journalist)" title="Robert Hunter (journalist)">Robert Hunter</a> and his wife Bobbi Hunter. Subsequently, the Stowe home at 2775 Courtenay Street became the headquarters.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Rex_Weyler" title="Rex Weyler">Rex Weyler</a> put it in his chronology, <i>Greenpeace</i>, in 1969, Irving and Dorothy Stowe's "quiet home on Courtenay Street would soon become a hub of monumental, global significance". Some of the first Greenpeace meetings were held there. The first office was opened in a backroom, storefront on Cypress and West Broadway SE corner in Kitsilano, Vancouver.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Within half a year Greenpeace would move in to share the upstairs office space with The Society Promoting Environmental Conservation at 4th and Maple in <a href="/wiki/Kitsilano" title="Kitsilano">Kitsilano</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Irving_Stowe" title="Irving Stowe">Irving Stowe</a> arranged a <a href="/wiki/Benefit_concert" title="Benefit concert">benefit concert</a> (supported by <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a>) that took place on October 16, 1970 at the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coliseum" title="Pacific Coliseum">Pacific Coliseum</a> in Vancouver. The concert created the financial basis for the first Greenpeace campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amchitka_(album)" title="Amchitka (album)">Amchitka, the 1970 concert that launched Greenpeace</a> was published by Greenpeace in November 2009 on CD and is also available as an mp3 download via the Amchitka concert website. Using the money raised with the concert, the Don't Make a Wave Committee chartered a ship, the <i>Phyllis Cormack</i> owned and sailed by John Cormack. The ship was renamed <i>Greenpeace</i> for the protest after a term coined by activist Bill Darnell.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the autumn of 1971, the ship sailed towards Amchitka and faced the U.S. Coast Guard ship <i><a href="/wiki/USCGC_Confidence_(WMEC-619)" title="USCGC Confidence (WMEC-619)">Confidence</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> which forced the activists to turn back. Because of this and the increasingly bad weather the crew decided to return to Canada only to find out that the news about their journey and reported support from the crew of the <i>Confidence</i> had generated sympathy for their protest.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> After this Greenpeace tried to navigate to the test site with other vessels, until the U.S. detonated the bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> The nuclear test was criticized and the U.S. decided not to continue with their test plans at Amchitka.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Founders_and_founding_time_of_Greenpeace">Founders and founding time of Greenpeace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Founders and founding time of Greenpeace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Environmental historian Frank Zelko dates the formation of the "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Make_a_Wave_Committee" title="Don't Make a Wave Committee">Don't Make a Wave Committee</a>" to 1969 and according to Jim Bohlen the group adopted the name "Don't Make a Wave Committee" on 28 November 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-zelko2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zelko2-34">[34]</a></sup> According to the Greenpeace web site, The Don't Make a Wave Committee was established in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenpeace:_founders_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace:_founders-35">[35]</a></sup> Certificate of incorporation of The Don't Make a Wave Committee dates the incorporation to the fifth of October, 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup> Researcher Vanessa Timmer dates the official incorporation to 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-Timmer_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timmer-37">[37]</a></sup> Greenpeace itself calls the protest voyage of 1971 as "the beginning".<sup id="cite_ref-The_History_of_Greenpeace_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_History_of_Greenpeace-38">[38]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Moore_(environmentalist)" title="Patrick Moore (environmentalist)">Patrick Moore</a>, who was an early member but has since distanced himself from Greenpeace, and Rex Weyler, the name of "The Don't Make a Wave Committee" was officially changed to Greenpeace Foundation in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weyler:chronology_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weyler:chronology-39">[39]</a></sup> Because of the early phases spanning several years, there are differing views on who can be called the founders of Greenpeace.</p>
<p>Vanessa Timmer has referred to the early members as "an unlikely group of loosely organized protestors".<sup id="cite_ref-Timmer_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timmer-37">[37]</a></sup> Frank Zelko has commented that "unlike <a href="/wiki/Friends_of_the_Earth" title="Friends of the Earth">Friends of the Earth</a>, for example, which sprung fully formed from the forehead of <a href="/wiki/David_Brower" title="David Brower">David Brower</a>, Greenpeace developed in a more evolutionary manner. There was no single founder".<sup id="cite_ref-zelko_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zelko-40">[40]</a></sup> Greenpeace itself says on its web page that "there's a joke that in any bar in Vancouver, Canada, you can sit down next to someone who claims to have founded Greenpeace. In fact, there was no single founder: name, idea, spirit and tactics can all be said to have separate lineages".<sup id="cite_ref-Greenpeace:_founders_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace:_founders-35">[35]</a></sup> Patrick Moore has said that "the truth is that Greenpeace was always a work in progress, not something definitively founded like a country or a company. Therefore there are a few shades of gray about who might lay claim to being a founder of Greenpeace."<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup> Early Greenpeace director <a href="/wiki/Rex_Weyler" title="Rex Weyler">Rex Weyler</a> says on his homepage that the insiders of Greenpeace have debated about the founders since mid-1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Weyler:founders_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weyler:founders-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The current Greenpeace web site lists the founders of The Don't Make a Wave Committee as Dorothy and Irving Stowe, Marie and Jim Bohlen, Ben and Dorothy Metcalfe, and Robert Hunter.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenpeace:_founders_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenpeace:_founders-35">[35]</a></sup> According to both Patrick Moore and an interview with Dorothy Stowe, Dorothy Metcalfe, Jim Bohlen and Robert Hunter, the founders of The Don't Make a Wave Committee were Paul Cote, Irving and Dorothy Stowe and Jim and Marie Bohlen.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Archive.greenpeace.org_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archive.greenpeace.org-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Watson" title="Paul Watson">Paul Watson</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Shepherd_Conservation_Society" title="Sea Shepherd Conservation Society">Sea Shepherd Conservation Society</a> maintains that he also was one of the founders of The Don't Make a Wave Committee and Greenpeace.<sup id="cite_ref-watson:founders_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watson:founders-43">[43]</a></sup> Media sources concerning Watson report him being one of the founders of Greenpeace,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> with many articles reporting him being a founder in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Patrick Moore has denied Watson being one of the founders of The Don't Make a Wave Committee, and Greenpeace in 1972. According to Moore the already campaigning organization was "simply changing the name" in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup> Greenpeace has stated that Watson was an influential early member, but not one of the founders of Greenpeace.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Watson has since criticized Greenpeace of rewriting their history.<sup id="cite_ref-watson:founders_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watson:founders-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because Patrick Moore was among the crew of the first protest voyage and the beginning of the journey is often referred as the birthday of Greenpeace, Moore also considers himself one of the founders.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore:_founders_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore:_founders-36">[36]</a></sup> Greenpeace used to list Moore among "founders and first members" of but has later stated that while Moore was a significant early member, he was not among the founders of Greenpeace in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Archive.greenpeace.org_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archive.greenpeace.org-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greenpeaceMoore_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeaceMoore-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_Amchitka">After Amchitka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: After Amchitka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the office in the Stowe home, (and after the first concert fund-raiser) Greenpeace functions moved to other private homes and held public meetings weekly on Wednesday nights at the Kitsilano Neighborhood House before settling, in the autumn of 1974, in a small office shared with the SPEC environmental group at 2007 West 4th at Maple in <a href="/wiki/Kitsilano" title="Kitsilano">Kitsilano</a>. When the nuclear tests at Amchitka were over, Greenpeace moved its focus to the French atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_testing" title="Nuclear weapons testing">nuclear weapons testing</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Moruroa" title="Moruroa">Moruroa Atoll</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a>. The young organization needed help for their protests and were contacted by <a href="/wiki/David_McTaggart" title="David McTaggart">David McTaggart</a>, a former businessman living in New Zealand. In 1972 the yacht <i>Vega</i>, a 12.5-metre (41&#160;ft) ketch owned by <a href="/wiki/David_McTaggart" title="David McTaggart">David McTaggart</a>, was renamed <i>Greenpeace III</i> and sailed in an anti-nuclear protest into the exclusion zone at Moruroa to attempt to disrupt French nuclear testing. This voyage was sponsored and organized by the New Zealand branch of the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French Navy</a> tried to stop the protest in several ways, including assaulting David McTaggart. McTaggart was supposedly beaten to the point that he lost sight in one of his eyes. However, one of McTaggart's crew members photographed the incident and went public. After the assault was publicized, France announced it would stop the atmospheric nuclear tests.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1970s some Greenpeace members started an independent campaign, Project Ahab, against commercial <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>, since Irving Stowe was against Greenpeace focusing on other issues than nuclear weapons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After Irving Stowe died in 1975, the <i>Phyllis Cormack</i> sailed from Vancouver to face Soviet whalers on the coast of California. Greenpeace activists disrupted the whaling by placing themselves between the harpoons and the whales, and footage of the protests spread across the world. Later in the 1970s, the organization widened its focus to include <a href="/wiki/Toxic_waste" title="Toxic waste">toxic waste</a> and commercial <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">seal hunting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The "Greenpeace <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Interdependence" title="Declaration of Interdependence">Declaration of Interdependence</a>" was published by Greenpeace in the <i>Greenpeace Chronicles</i> (Winter 1976-77). This declaration was a condensation of a number of ecological manifestos <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hunter_(journalist)" title="Robert Hunter (journalist)">Bob Hunter</a> had written over the years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_development">Organizational development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Organizational development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg/220px-Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg/330px-Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg/440px-Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3269" data-file-height="1813" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greenpeace_ship_%22Esperanza%22_off_Gravesend.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/MV_Esperanza" title="MV Esperanza">MV <i>Esperanza</i></a>, a former fire-fighter owned by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Navy" title="Russian Navy">Russian Navy</a>, was relaunched by Greenpeace in 2002</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Greenpeace evolved from a group of Canadian and American protesters into a less conservative group of environmentalists who were more reflective of the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> youth movements of the 1960s and 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The social and cultural background from which Greenpeace emerged heralded a period of de-conditioning away from Old World antecedents and sought to develop new codes of social, environmental and political behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-odysseu_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odysseu-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1970s independent groups using the name Greenpeace started springing up worldwide. By 1977 there were 15 to 20 Greenpeace groups around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace._56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace.-56">[56]</a></sup> At the same time the Canadian Greenpeace office was heavily in debt. Disputes between offices over fund-raising and organizational direction split the global movement as the North American offices were reluctant to be under the authority of the Vancouver office and its president <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Moore_(environmentalist)" title="Patrick Moore (environmentalist)">Patrick Moore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace._56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace.-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the incidents of Moruroa Atoll, David McTaggart had moved to France to battle in court with the French state and helped to develop the cooperation of European Greenpeace groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story_29-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brown_.26_John_May:_The_Greenpeace_Story-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_McTaggart" title="David McTaggart">David McTaggart</a> lobbied the Canadian Greenpeace Foundation to accept a new structure which would bring the scattered Greenpeace offices under the auspices of a single global organization. The European Greenpeace paid the debt of the Canadian Greenpeace office and on October 14, 1979, <b>Greenpeace International</b> came into existence.<sup id="cite_ref-Timmer_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Timmer-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace._56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace.-56">[56]</a></sup> Under the new structure, the local offices would contribute a percentage of their income to the international organization, which would take responsibility for setting the overall direction of the movement with each regional office having one vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace._56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waves_of_Compassion._The_founding_of_Greenpeace.-56">[56]</a></sup> Some Greenpeace groups, namely <a href="/wiki/London_Greenpeace" title="London Greenpeace">London Greenpeace</a> (dissolved in 2001) and the US-based <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace_Foundation" title="Greenpeace Foundation">Greenpeace Foundation</a> (still operational) however decided to remain independent from Greenpeace International.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_structure">Organizational structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Organizational structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GPIgovernance%26management.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GPIgovernance%26management.png/300px-GPIgovernance%26management.png" width="300" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GPIgovernance%26management.png/450px-GPIgovernance%26management.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/GPIgovernance%26management.png/600px-GPIgovernance%26management.png 2x" data-file-width="1229" data-file-height="967" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GPIgovernance%26management.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The governance and management structure of Greenpeace.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Greenpeace consists of <i>Greenpeace International</i> (officially Stichting Greenpeace Council) based in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, and 26 regional offices operating in 55 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-organization_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organization-59">[59]</a></sup> The regional offices work largely autonomously under the supervision of Greenpeace International. The executive director of Greenpeace is elected by the board members of Greenpeace International. The current directors of Greenpeace International are Bunny McDiarmaid and Jennifer Morgan and the current Chair of the Board is Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Ana_Toni" title="Ana Toni">Ana Toni</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-executive_director_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-executive_director-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Board_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Board-61">[61]</a></sup> Greenpeace has a staff of 2,400<sup id="cite_ref-Annual_report_2008_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual_report_2008-62">[62]</a></sup> and 15,000 volunteers globally.<sup id="cite_ref-involved_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-involved-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each regional office is led by a regional executive director elected by the regional board of directors. The regional boards also appoint a trustee to The Greenpeace International Annual General Meeting, where the trustees elect or remove the board of directors of Greenpeace International. The role of the annual general meeting is also to discuss and decide the overall principles and strategically important issues for Greenpeace in collaboration with the trustees of regional offices and Greenpeace International board of directors.<sup id="cite_ref-governance_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-governance-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding">Funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Funding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greenpeace receives its funding from individual supporters and foundations.<sup id="cite_ref-GPI-FAQ_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPI-FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBS_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBS-7">[7]</a></sup> Greenpeace screens all major donations in order to ensure it does not receive unwanted donations.<sup id="cite_ref-funding_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-funding-64">[64]</a></sup> The organization does not accept money from governments, intergovernmental organizations, political parties or corporations in order to avoid their influence.<sup id="cite_ref-GPI-FAQ_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPI-FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBS_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBS-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-funding_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-funding-64">[64]</a></sup> However, Greenpeace does receive money from the National Postcode Lottery, the biggest government-sponsored lottery in the Netherlands,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and several for profit companies like <a href="/wiki/Ben_%26_Jerry%27s" title="Ben &amp; Jerry's">Ben &amp; Jerry's</a> partner with and indicate they donate a percentage of sales to Greenpeace campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Donations from foundations which are funded by political parties or receive most of their funding from governments or intergovernmental organizations are rejected. Foundation donations are also rejected if the foundations attach unreasonable conditions, restrictions or constraints on Greenpeace activities or if the donation would compromise the independence and aims of Greenpeace.<sup id="cite_ref-funding_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-funding-64">[64]</a></sup> Since in the mid-1990s the number of supporters started to decrease, Greenpeace pioneered the use of <a href="/wiki/Street_fundraiser" class="mw-redirect" title="Street fundraiser">face-to-face fundraising</a> where fundraisers actively seek new supporters at public places, subscribing them for a monthly direct debit donation.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In 2008, most of the €202.5 million received by the organization was donated by about 2.6 million regular supporters, mainly from Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Annual_report_2008_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Annual_report_2008-62">[62]</a></sup> In 2014, the annual revenue of Greenpeace was reported to be about €300 million (US$400 million) although they lost about €4 million (US$5 million) in currency speculation that year.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DDC-GP.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/DDC-GP.JPG/220px-DDC-GP.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/DDC-GP.JPG/330px-DDC-GP.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/DDC-GP.JPG/440px-DDC-GP.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DDC-GP.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Greenpeace street fundraiser talking to a passer-by.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 2003, <a href="/wiki/Public_Interest_Watch" title="Public Interest Watch">Public Interest Watch</a> (PIW) complained to the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> that Greenpeace USA tax returns were inaccurate and in violation of the law.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The IRS conducted an extensive review and concluded in December 2005 that Greenpeace USA continued to qualify for its tax-exempt status. In March 2006 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> reported that PIW's "federal tax filing, covering August 2003 to July 2004, stated that $120,000 of the $124,095 the group received in contributions during that period came from Exxon Mobil."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In 2013, after the IRS performed a follow-up audit, which again was clean, and, following claims of politically motivated IRS audits of groups affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Tea Party Movement">Tea Party Movement</a>, Greenpeace U.S. Executive Director <a href="/wiki/Phil_Radford" title="Phil Radford">Phil Radford</a> called for a Congressional investigation into all politically motivated audits – including those allegedly targeting the Tea Party Movement, the <a href="/wiki/NAACP" class="mw-redirect" title="NAACP">NAACP</a>, and Greenpeace.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary_of_priorities_and_campaigns">Summary of priorities and campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Summary of priorities and campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On its official website, Greenpeace defines its mission as the following:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Greenpeace is an independent global <a href="/wiki/Civil_society_campaign" title="Civil society campaign">campaigning</a> organization that acts to change attitudes and behaviour, to protect and conserve the environment and to promote <a href="/wiki/Peace" title="Peace">peace</a> by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Catalysing an energy revolution to address the number one threat facing our planet: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.</li>
<li>Defending our oceans by challenging wasteful and destructive <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>, and creating a global network of <a href="/wiki/Marine_reserve" title="Marine reserve">marine reserves</a>.</li>
<li>Protecting the world's remaining <a href="/wiki/Ancient_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient forest">ancient forests</a> which are depended on by many animals, plants and people.</li>
<li>Working for <a href="/wiki/Disarmament" title="Disarmament">disarmament</a> and peace by reducing dependence on finite resources and calling for the elimination of all <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>.</li>
<li>Creating a toxin free future with safer alternatives to hazardous <a href="/wiki/Chemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemicals">chemicals</a> in today's products and manufacturing.</li>
<li>Campaigning for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a> by encouraging socially and ecologically responsible farming practices.
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Greenpeace International<sup id="cite_ref-who_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who-74">[74]</a></sup></cite></div>
</li>
</ul>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_and_energy">Climate and energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate and energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Greenpeace was one of the first parties to formulate a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> scenario for climate change mitigation, which it did in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> According to sociologists Marc Mormont and Christine Dasnoy, Greenpeace played a significant role in raising public awareness of global warming in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The organization has also focused on <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>, because of both their global warming potential and their effect on the ozone layer. Greenpeace was one of the leading participants advocating early phase-out of ozone depleting substances in the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol-16">[16]</a></sup> In the early 1990s, Greenpeace developed a CFC-free refrigerator technology, "Greenfreeze" for mass production together with the refrigerator industry.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol-16">[16]</a></sup> United Nations Environment Programme awarded Greenpeace for "outstanding contributions to the protection of the Earth's ozone layer" in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 2011 two fifths of the world's total production of refrigerators were based on Greenfreeze technology, with over 600 million units in use.<sup id="cite_ref-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP:_Our_Planet:_Celebrating_20_Years_of_Montreal_Protocol-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace.org_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace.org-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Currently Greenpeace considers <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> to be the greatest environmental problem facing the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-who_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who-74">[74]</a></sup> Greenpeace calls for global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions to peak in 2015 and to decrease as close to zero as possible by 2050. For this Greenpeace calls for the industrialized countries to cut their emissions at least 40% by 2020 (from 1990 levels) and to give substantial funding for developing countries to build a sustainable energy capacity, to adapt to the inevitable consequences of global warming, and to stop <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Together with <a href="/wiki/EREC" class="mw-redirect" title="EREC">EREC</a>, Greenpeace has formulated a global energy scenario, "Energy [R]evolution", where 80% of the world's total energy is produced with renewables, and the emissions of the energy sector are decreased by over 80% of the 1990 levels by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace1_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace1-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Using direct action, Greenpeace has protested several times against coal by occupying coal power plants and blocking coal shipments and mining operations, in places such as New Zealand,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Greenpeace is also critical of extracting petroleum from <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a> and has used direct action to block the oil sand operations at <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" class="mw-redirect" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingsnorth_court_case">Kingsnorth court case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Kingsnorth court case">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2007, six Greenpeace protesters were arrested for breaking into the <a href="/wiki/Kingsnorth_power_station" title="Kingsnorth power station">Kingsnorth power station</a>, climbing the 200 metre smokestack, painting the name <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon</a> on the chimney, and causing an estimated £30,000 damage. At their subsequent trial they admitted trying to shut the station down, but argued that they were legally justified because they were trying to prevent climate change from causing greater damage to property elsewhere around the world. Evidence was heard from <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>'s environment adviser <a href="/wiki/Zac_Goldsmith" title="Zac Goldsmith">Zac Goldsmith</a>, climate scientist <a href="/wiki/James_E._Hansen" class="mw-redirect" title="James E. Hansen">James E. Hansen</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> leader from Greenland, all saying that climate change was already seriously affecting life around the world. The six activists were acquitted. It was the first case where preventing property damage caused by climate change has been used as part of a "lawful excuse" defense in court.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a> described the acquittal as embarrassment to the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown Ministry">Brown Ministry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In December 2008 <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> listed the acquittal in its annual list of the most influential ideas of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Go_Beyond_Oil.22">"Go Beyond Oil"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: &quot;Go Beyond Oil&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of their stance on <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable energy commercialisation">renewable energy commercialisation</a>, Greenpeace have launched the "Go Beyond Oil" campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-GoBeyondOil_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoBeyondOil-91">[91]</a></sup> The campaign is focused on slowing, and eventually ending, the world's consumption of oil; with activist activities taking place against companies that pursue oil drilling as a venture. Much of the activities of the "Go Beyond Oil" campaign have been focused on drilling for oil in the Arctic and areas affected by the <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon" title="Deepwater Horizon">Deepwater Horizon</a> disaster. The activities of Greenpeace in the arctic have mainly involved the Edinburgh-based oil and gas exploration company, Cairn Energy; and range from protests at the Cairn Energy's headquarters<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> to scaling their oil rigs in an attempt to halt the drilling process.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>The "Go Beyond Oil" campaign also involves applying political pressure on the governments who allow oil exploration in their territories; with the group stating that one of the key aims of the "Go Beyond Oil" campaign is to "work to expose the lengths the oil industry is willing to go to squeeze the last barrels out of the ground and put pressure on industry and governments to move beyond oil."<sup id="cite_ref-GoBeyondOil_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoBeyondOil-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">Nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;width:18.5em;">
<tr>
<th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#f0f0f0;"><a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Anti-nuclear movement</a></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em">
<div class="center">
