<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anti-nuclear_movement_sidebar" title="Template:Anti-nuclear movement sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Anti-nuclear_movement_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Anti-nuclear movement sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anti-nuclear_movement_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Greenpeace is opposed to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> because it views it is 'dangerous, polluting, expensive and non-renewable'. The organization highlights the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster of 1986</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster of 2011</a> as evidence of the risk nuclear power can pose to peoples lives, the environment and the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Greenpeace views the benefits of nuclear power to be relatively minor in comparison to its major problems and risks, such as environmental damage and risks from uranium mining, nuclear weapons proliferation, and unresolved questions concerning nuclear waste.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The organization argues that the potential of nuclear power to <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate global warming</a> is marginal, referring to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> energy scenario where an increase in world's nuclear capacity from 2608 TWh in 2007 to 9857 TWh by 2050 would cut global greenhouse gas emissions less than 5% and require 32 nuclear reactor units of 1000MW capacity built per year until 2050. According to Greenpeace the slow construction times, construction delays, and hidden costs, all negate the mitigation potential of nuclear power. This makes the IEA scenario technically and financially unrealistic. They also argue that binding massive amounts of investments on nuclear energy would take funding away from more effective solutions.<sup id="cite_ref-greenpeace1_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenpeace1-80">[80]</a></sup> Greenpeace views the construction of <a href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor#Olkiluoto_3_pilot_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="European Pressurized Reactor">Olkiluoto 3</a> nuclear power plant in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> as an example of the problems on building new nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-waste_of_time_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-waste_of_time-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-nuclear_advertisement">Anti-nuclear advertisement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anti-nuclear advertisement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1994, Greenpeace published an anti-nuclear newspaper advert which included a claim that nuclear facilities <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Sellafield</a> would kill 2,000 people in the next 10 years, and an image of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a>-affected child said to be a victim of <a href="/wiki/Semipalatinsk_Test_Site" title="Semipalatinsk Test Site">nuclear weapons testing in Kazakhstan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Advertising_Standards_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Advertising Standards Authority (United Kingdom)">Advertising Standards Authority</a> viewed the claim concerning Sellafield unsubstantiated, and ASA did not accept that the child's condition was caused by radiation. This resulted in banning of the advert. Greenpeace did not admit fault, stating that a Kazakhstan doctor had said that the child's condition was due to nuclear testing. Adam Woolf from Greenpeace also stated that, "fifty years ago there were many experts who would be lined up and swear there was no link between smoking and bad health."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The UN has estimated that the nuclear weapon tests in Kazakhstan caused about 100,000 people to suffer over three generations.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="EDF_spying_conviction_and_appeal">EDF spying conviction and appeal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: EDF spying conviction and appeal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2011, a French court fined <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lectricit%C3%A9_de_France" title="Électricité de France">Électricité de France</a> (EDF) €1.5m and jailed two senior employees for spying on Greenpeace, including hacking into Greenpeace's computer systems. Greenpeace was awarded €500,000 in damages.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Although EDF claimed that a security firm had only been employed to monitor Greenpeace, the court disagreed, jailing the head and deputy head of EDF's nuclear security operation for three years each. EDF appealed the conviction, the company was cleared of conspiracy to spy on Greenpeace and the fine was cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Two employees of the security firm, Kargus, run by a former member of France's secret services, received sentences of three and two years respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cool_IT_Leaderboard">Cool IT Leaderboard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cool IT Leaderboard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 2009, Greenpeace started evaluating IT companies through Cool IT Leaderboard. The central role these companies are playing gives the opportunity to drive changes in the energy sector and achieve a significant reduction in the greenhouse gases that cause the climate change.</p>
<p>The Leaderboard examines how the leading IT companies can use their influence to drive these changes, which in the 6th edition the sector shows slow but steady improvements in offering new solution that may achieve important progress. There are companies willing to make major investments to drive clean energy deployment and the number of companies that are increasing their commitment, betting for renewable energies, is also growing.</p>
<p>Big companies such as Google, Wipro, Sprint and Softbank have prioritized changing the laws and policies that govern the energy system and incentivise investments in advanced energy efficiency technologies and renewable energy that will have a critical impact in many countries to determine a meaningful break in energy policies.</p>
<p>In the Leaderboard we can find one main ranking with the overall scores and another three rankings for each one of the evaluated areas where we can find the top companies contributing in each.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_Layer_and_Greenfreeze">Ozone Layer and Greenfreeze</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ozone Layer and Greenfreeze">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Ozone layers surrounding the Earth absorb significant amounts of ultraviolet radiation. A 1976 report by the US Academy of Sciences supported the ozone "depletion hypothesis."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Its suffering large losses from chlorinated and nitrogenous compounds was reported in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Earlier studies had led some countries to enact bans on aerosol sprays, so that the Vienna Convention was signed in 1985<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> the Montreal Protocol was signed in 1987 to go in force two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The use of CFC's and HCFC's in refrigeration were and are among the banned technologies. A German technological institute developed an ozone-safe hydrocarbon alternative refrigerant which came to a Greenpeace campaigner's attention in around 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The rights to the technology were donated to Greenpeace, which maintained it as an open source patent. With industry resistance, Greenpeace was able to rescue and engage a former East German manufacturer near closure. Greenpeace's resourceful outreach and marketing resulted in the technologies rapid widespread production in Germany, followed by the banning of CFC technology. They then succeeded in getting Greenfreeze used in China and elsewhere in Europe, and after some years in Japan and South America, and finally in the US by 2012.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_campaign">Forest campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Forest campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Greenpeace aims at protecting intact primary forests from <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and degradation with the target of zero deforestation by 2020. Greenpeace has accused several corporations, such as <a href="/wiki/Unilever" title="Unilever">Unilever</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/KFC" title="KFC">KFC</a>, <a href="/wiki/KitKat" class="mw-redirect" title="KitKat">KitKat</a> and <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> of having links to the deforestation of the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>, resulting in policy changes in several of the companies under criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-Amazon_leather_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amazon_leather-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lop_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lop-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Greenpeace, together with other environmental <a href="/wiki/NGO" class="mw-redirect" title="NGO">NGOs</a>, also campaigned for ten years for the EU to ban import of illegal timber. The EU decided to ban illegal timber on July 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> As deforestation contributes to global warming, Greenpeace has demanded that <a href="/wiki/REDD" class="mw-redirect" title="REDD">REDD</a> (Reduced Emission from Deforestation and Forest Degradation) should be included in the climate treaty following the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Kyoto treaty">Kyoto treaty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another Greenpeace movement concerning the rain forests is discouraging palm oil industries.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The movement has been the most active in Indonesia where already 6 million hectares are used for palm oil plantation and has plans for another 4 million hectares by 2015. Acknowledging that mass production of palm oil may be disastrous on biodiversity of forests, Greenpeace is actively campaigning against the production, urging the industries and the government to turn to other forms of energy resources. One of the positive results of the campaign was GAR(Golden Agri-Resources),<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> the world's second largest palm oil production company, deciding to commit itself to forest conservation. The company signed an agreement which prevents them from developing plantations in areas where large amounts of carbon are locked up.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_of_an_ancient_tree">Removal of an ancient tree</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Removal of an ancient tree">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 1995, Greenpeace took a trunk of a tree from the forests of the proposed <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national park</a> of Koitajoki<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Ilomantsi" title="Ilomantsi">Ilomantsi</a>, Finland and put it on display at exhibitions held in Austria and Germany. Greenpeace said in a press conference that the tree was originally from a logged area in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient forest">ancient forest</a> which was supposed to be protected. <a href="/wiki/Mets%C3%A4hallitus" title="Metsähallitus">Metsähallitus</a> accused Greenpeace of theft and said that the tree was from a normal forest and had been left standing because of its old age. Metsähallitus also said that the tree had actually crashed over a road during a storm.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The incident received publicity in Finland, for example in the large newspapers <i><a href="/wiki/Helsingin_sanomat" class="mw-redirect" title="Helsingin sanomat">Helsingin Sanomat</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ilta-Sanomat" title="Ilta-Sanomat">Ilta-Sanomat</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Greenpeace replied that the tree had fallen down because the protective forest around it had been <a href="/wiki/Clearcutting" title="Clearcutting">clearcut</a>, and that they wanted to highlight the fate of old forests in general, not the fate of one particular tree.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Greenpeace also highlighted that Metsähallitus admitted the value of the forest afterwards as Metsähallitus currently refers to Koitajoki as a distinctive area because of its old growth forests.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tokyo_Two">Tokyo Two</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tokyo Two">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Two" title="Tokyo Two">Tokyo Two</a></div>
<p>In 2008, two Greenpeace anti-whaling activists, Junichi Sato and Toru Suzuki, stole a case of <a href="/wiki/Whale_meat" title="Whale meat">whale meat</a> from a delivery depot in Aomori prefecture, Japan. Their intention was to expose what they considered embezzlement of the meat collected during whale hunts. After a brief investigation of their allegations was ended, Sato and Suzuki were arrested and charged with theft and trespass.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> said that the arrests and following raids on Greenpeace Japan office and homes of five of Greenpeace staff members were aimed at intimidating activists and non-governmental organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> They were convicted of theft and trespass in September 2010 by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aomori_district_court&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aomori district court (page does not exist)">Aomori district court</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetically_modified_organisms_.28GMOs.29">Genetically modified organisms (GMOs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Genetically modified organisms (GMOs)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Greenpeace has also supported the rejection of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">GM food</a> from the US in famine-stricken Zambia as long as supplies of non-genetically engineered grain exist, stating that the US "should follow in the European Union's footsteps and allow aid recipients to choose their food aid, buying it locally if they wish. This practice can stimulate developing economies and creates more robust food security", adding that, "if Africans truly have no other alternative, the controversial GE maize should be milled so it can't be planted. It was this condition that allowed Zambia's neighbours Zimbabwe and Malawi to accept it."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> After Zambia banned all GM food aid, the former agricultural minister of Zambia criticized, "how the various international NGOs that have spoken approvingly of the government's action will square the body count with their various consciences."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Concerning the decision of Zambia, Greenpeace has stated that, "it was obvious to us that if no non-GM aid was being offered then they should absolutely accept GM food aid. But the Zambian government decided to refuse the GM food. We offered our opinion to the Zambian government and, as many governments do, they disregarded our advice."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenpeace_on_golden_rice">Greenpeace on golden rice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greenpeace on golden rice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greenpeace opposes the planned use of <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">golden rice</a>, a variety of <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> produced through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> to <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesize</a> <a href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>, a precursor of pro-<a href="/wiki/Retinol" title="Retinol">vitamin A</a> in the edible parts of rice. The addition of beta-carotene to the rice is seen as preventative to loss of sight in poverty stricken countries where golden rice is intended for distribution. According to Greenpeace, golden rice has not managed to do anything about malnutrition for 10 years during which alternative methods are already tackling malnutrition. The alternative proposed by Greenpeace is to discourage <a href="/wiki/Mono-cropping" class="mw-redirect" title="Mono-cropping">mono-cropping</a> and to increase production of crops which are naturally nutrient-rich (containing other nutrients not found in golden rice in addition to beta-<a href="/wiki/Carotene" title="Carotene">carotene</a>). Greenpeace argues that resources should be spent on programs that are already working and helping to relieve malnutrition.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The renewal of these concerns coincided with the publication of a paper in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> about a version of golden rice with much higher levels of beta carotene.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> This "golden rice 2" was developed and patented by <a href="/wiki/Syngenta" title="Syngenta">Syngenta</a>, which provoked Greenpeace to renew its allegation that the project is driven by <a href="/wiki/Profit_motive" title="Profit motive">profit motives</a> and to serve as propaganda aimed at increasing public opinion of GMO products.<sup id="cite_ref-ns_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ns-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Greenpeace had stated that the true efficiency of the golden rice program in treating malnourished populations was its primary concern as early as 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> statements from March and April 2005 also continued to express concern over human health and environmental safety.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In particular, Greenpeace has expressed concern over the lack of safety testing being done on GMO crops such as golden rice and of "playing with the lives of people...using Golden Rice to promote more GMOs".<sup id="cite_ref-ns_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ns-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2016, a conglomeration of 107 Nobel Laureates signed an open letter<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[21]</a></sup> urging Greenpeace to end its campaign against genetically modified crops and Golden Rice in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> In the letter, they also called upon governments of the world to "do everything in their power to oppose Greenpeace's actions and accelerate the access of farmers to all the tools of modern biology, especially seeds improved through biotechnology." The letter states that "Opposition based on emotion and dogma contradicted by data must be stopped."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">[21]</a></sup> Greenpeace responded stating that "Accusations that anyone is blocking genetically engineered 'Golden' rice are false" and that they support "...investing in climate-resilient ecological agriculture and empowering farmers to access a balanced and nutritious diet, rather than pouring money down the drain for GE 'Golden' rice."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxic_waste">Toxic waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Toxic waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In July 2011, Greenpeace released its Dirty Laundry report accusing some of the world's top fashion and <a href="/wiki/Sportswear_(activewear)" title="Sportswear (activewear)">sportswear</a> brands of releasing <a href="/wiki/Toxic_waste" title="Toxic waste">toxic waste</a> into China's rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The report profiles the problem of <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> resulting from the release of toxic chemicals associated with the country's <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textile industry</a>. Investigations focused on <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> discharges from two facilities in China; one belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Youngor_Group" title="Youngor Group">Youngor Group</a> located on the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_Delta" title="Yangtze River Delta">Yangtze River Delta</a> and the other to Well Dyeing Factory Ltd. located on a tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a>. Scientific analysis of samples from both facilities revealed the presence of hazardous and persistent <a href="/wiki/Hormone_disruptor" class="mw-redirect" title="Hormone disruptor">hormone disruptor</a> chemicals, including <a href="/wiki/Alkylphenol" title="Alkylphenol">alkylphenols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perfluorinated_compound" title="Perfluorinated compound">perfluorinated compounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perfluorooctane_sulfonate" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfluorooctane sulfonate">perfluorooctane sulfonate</a>.</p>
<p>The report goes on to assert that the Youngor Group and Well Dyeing Factory Ltd. - the two companies behind the facilities - have commercial relationships with a range of major clothing brands, including <a href="/wiki/Abercrombie_%26_Fitch" title="Abercrombie &amp; Fitch">Abercrombie &amp; Fitch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adidas" title="Adidas">Adidas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bauer_Hockey" title="Bauer Hockey">Bauer Hockey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Klein" title="Calvin Klein">Calvin Klein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Converse_(shoe_company)" title="Converse (shoe company)">Converse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cortefiel" title="Cortefiel">Cortefiel</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%26M" title="H&amp;M">H&amp;M</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lacoste" title="Lacoste">Lacoste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Li_Ning_(company)" class="mw-redirect" title="Li Ning (company)">Li Ning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metersbonwe_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Metersbonwe Group">Metersbonwe Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phillips-Van_Heusen" class="mw-redirect" title="Phillips-Van Heusen">Phillips-Van Heusen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puma_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Puma AG">Puma AG</a>.</p>
<p>In 2013, Greenpeace launched the "Detox Fashion" campaign, which signed up some fashion brands to stop the discharge of toxic chemicals into rivers as a result of the production of their clothes.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Guide_to_Greener_Electronics">Guide to Greener Electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Guide to Greener Electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One important issue nowadays is the fact that everyone relies more in electronic gadgets to make their lives more productive and fun, but the rate we purchase and discard these products is having an important impact in our planet. In August 2006, Greenpeace released the first edition of Guide to Greener Electronics, a magazine where the companies were ranked for their green performance, mainly based in the use of toxics in their products and the e-waste.</p>
<p>The first edition of the magazine came up in August 2006 ranking the leading mobile and PC manufacturers.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The criteria used reflected the demands of the Toxic Tech campaign where they demanded to the electronics companies to make products without toxic substances and take them back and recycle them once they are not going to be used any longer. In November 2011, the criteria were updated, as the industry had progressed since 2006, with the objective to get companies setting ambitious goals for greenhouse gas reduction, the use of renewable power up to 100 percent, producing long lasting products free of hazardous substances and increasing sustainable practices across the global electronics supply chain.</p>
<p>Every three months, Greenpeace released a new updated edition of the guide. The companies selected for every edition are determined by the latest industry sales figures of global market share for the past year in the electronics sector (mobile devices, PC and TV). The high rate of innovation in those industries makes them also highly competitive, therefore, by publicly comparing their performance, the guide encourage to make use of best practices to address the most important environmental challenges and also provides the customer valuable information of the impact that the company has to their environment.</p>
<p>In order to ensure the transparency of the ranking the companies are assessed based only on their public information. For proving companies' policies and practices, Greenpeace uses chemical testing of products, reports from industry observers, media reports and testing of consumer programs to check if they match with their actions. In case of contradiction of the company policy, are published and clearly explained the corresponding penalty points.</p>
<p>Since the Guide was released in 2006, along with other similar campaigns has driven numerous improvements. Many of the companies ranked take the results very seriously and try to improve their performances such as eliminating toxic chemicals from their products and improve their recycling schemes.</p>
<p>The last published edition of Guide to Greener Electronics was the 18th, in November 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Save_the_Arctic">Save the Arctic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Save the Arctic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Arctic" title="Save the Arctic">Save the Arctic</a></div>
<p>In continuity of the successful campaign to reach the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic-Environmental_Protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Antarctic-Environmental Protocol">Antarctic-Environmental Protocol</a>, in 2012 and 2013 protests with "Save the Arctic" banners were started. To stop oil- and gas-drilling, industrial fishing and military operations in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> region completely, a "global sanctuary in the high arctic" was demanded from the World leaders at the UN General Assembly: "We want them to pass a UN resolution expressing international concern for the Arctic." A resolution to protect the very vulnerable wildlife and ecosystem.<sup id="cite_ref-savethearctic2013_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-savethearctic2013-144">[144]</a></sup> 30 <i>Arctic Sunrise</i> activists were <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace_Arctic_Sunrise_ship_case" title="Greenpeace Arctic Sunrise ship case">arrested in the Pechora Sea</a>, 19 September 2013, witnessing oil-drilling and protesting at the Gazprom <a href="/wiki/Prirazlomnaya_platform" class="mw-redirect" title="Prirazlomnaya platform">platform Prirazlomnaya</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Coast_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Coast Guard">Russian Coast Guard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spiegel09-20-2013_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spiegel09-20-2013-145">[145]</a></sup> Greenpeace members were originally charged with Piracy, then later downgraded to hooliganism, before being dropped altogether following the passage of an amnesty law by the Russian government.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2014, Greenpeace launched a global boycott campaign to persuade <a href="/wiki/Lego_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Lego Group">Lego</a> to cease producing toys carrying the oil company Shell's logo in response to Shell's plans to drill for oil in the Arctic.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Lego's partnership with Shell dates back to the 1960s, although the LEGO company created a fictional oil company called <a href="/wiki/Octan" title="Octan">Octan</a>. Octan has appeared in countless sets, computer and console games, can be seen at <a href="/wiki/Legoland" title="Legoland">Legoland</a> parks, and is featured as the corporation headed by the villain President Business in <a href="/wiki/The_Lego_Movie" title="The Lego Movie">The Lego Movie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Norway">In Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a conflict over oil rigs in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Norwegian Government">Norwegian Government</a> and Greenpeace. In 2013, three activists of Greenpeace got on a <a href="/wiki/Statoil" title="Statoil">Statoil</a>'s oil rig, wearing bear suits. According to a spokesman from Greenpeace <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, they stayed on the rig for about three hours. The activists in bear suits "were escorted" to the shore. <a href="/wiki/Statoil" title="Statoil">Statoil</a> reportedly did not intend to file a suit against them.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>Greenpeace had argued that Statoil's drilling plans posed a threat to <a href="/wiki/Bear_Island_(Norway)" title="Bear Island (Norway)">Bear Island</a>, an uninhabited wildlife sanctuary that is home to rare species including polar bears, because an oil spill would be nearly impossible to clean up in the Arctic because of the harsh conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Greenpeace regards the petroleum activities of Statoil as "illegal".<sup id="cite_ref-Hovland1_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hovland1-151">[151]</a></sup> Statoil denies the Greenpeace statement. According to The Maritime Executive (2014),<sup id="cite_ref-Maritime1_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maritime1-152">[152]</a></sup> Statoil says "Statoil respects people's right to make a legal protest, and we feel it is important to have a democratic debate around the oil industry. We have established robust plans for the operation, and feel confident they can be carried out safely and without accidents."</p>
<p>On May 27, 2014, Greenpeace's ship, <a href="/wiki/MV_Esperanza" title="MV Esperanza">MV <i>Esperanza</i></a>, took over <a href="/wiki/Transocean" title="Transocean">Transocean</a> <a href="/wiki/Spitsbergen" title="Spitsbergen">Spitsbergen</a>, oil rig of <a href="/wiki/Statoil" title="Statoil">Statoil</a><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> in the Barents Sea such that it became incapable of operating. After that, the manager of Greenpeace Norway <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> Truls Gulowsen answered a phone interview, stating that "Five protesters left the rig by helicopter last night and three returned to a nearby Greenpeace ship."<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-154">[154]</a></sup> There were seven more protesters on the rig at the time, but the Norwegian police could not remove them immediately because the rig was a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_convenience" title="Flag of convenience">flag of convenience</a> ship registered in the Marshall Islands and thus regarded as a ship in the open sea, as long as it did not begin drilling. On May 29, however, the seven activists from Greenpeace were peacefully captured by Norwegian police on the rig. Soon after, according to Reuters, all the activists were set free without any fine. On May 30, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Coast_Guard" title="Norwegian Coast Guard">Norwegian Coast Guard</a> finally towed away <i>Esperanza</i>, though in the morning Greenpeace submitted a plea on which more than 80,000 signatures to the Norwegian Environment Minister <a href="/wiki/Tine_Sundtoft" title="Tine Sundtoft">Tine Sundtoft</a> in Oslo were written. Norwegian government and police reportedly allowed the coast guard to tow the Greenpeace ship.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwaymoves_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwaymoves-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Norwegian police stated that Statoil asked Greenpeace to stop preventing its activities, but Greenpeace ignored the warning. The police have stated that Greenpeace's interference with the petroleum activities of Statoil was the contrary to Norwegian law and ordered Greenpeace to leave the Barents Sea site.<sup id="cite_ref-Hovland1_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hovland1-151">[151]</a></sup> Statoil said delays to the start of drilling cost the company about $1.26 million per day.<sup id="cite_ref-Norwaymoves_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norwaymoves-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Reuters, Statoil was slated to begin drilling "three oil wells in the Apollo, Atlantis and Mercury prospects in the Hoop area, [which is] some 300 km away from the mainland [of Norway]" in the summer of 2014. Greenpeace has continued to criticize the big oil company for their "<a href="/wiki/Green_wash" class="mw-redirect" title="Green wash">green wash</a>," arguing that Statoil hid the truth that it is doing the risky oil drilling by holding "<a href="/wiki/Lego_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Lego League">Lego League</a>" with Lego and distracting people's attention to the company's project, and it also argues that Statoil has to alter its attitude toward environments<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> (Norway's News in English 2014).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ships">Ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenpeace&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since Greenpeace was founded, seagoing ships have played a vital role in its campaigns.</p>
